<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wernher_von_Braun&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Wernher von Braun was born on 23 March 1912 in the small town of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wirsitz" title="Wirsitz">Wirsitz</a>, in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Posen_Province" title="Posen Province">Posen Province</a>, of the former German Empire. He was the second of three sons. He belonged to a <a href="/wiki/Nobility" title="Nobility">noble</a> family, inheriting the German title of <i><a href="/wiki/Freiherr" title="Freiherr">Freiherr</a></i> (equivalent to <a href="/wiki/Baron" title="Baron">Baron</a>).</p>
<p>His father, conservative civil servant <a href="/wiki/Magnus_von_Braun_(senior)" title="Magnus von Braun (senior)">Magnus Freiherr von Braun</a> (1878–1972), served as a Minister of Agriculture in the Reich Cabinet during the <a href="/wiki/Weimar_Republic" title="Weimar Republic">Weimar Republic</a>. His mother, Emmy von Quistorp (1886–1959), could trace her ancestry through both parents to medieval European <a href="/wiki/Royal_family" title="Royal family">royalty</a> and was a descendant of <a href="/wiki/Philip_III_of_France" title="Philip III of France">Philip III of France</a>, <a href="/wiki/Valdemar_I_of_Denmark" title="Valdemar I of Denmark">Valdemar I of Denmark</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_III_of_Scotland" title="Robert III of Scotland">Robert III of Scotland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Edward_III_of_England" title="Edward III of England">Edward III of England</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Von Braun had an older brother, <a href="/wiki/Sigismund_von_Braun" title="Sigismund von Braun">Sigismund</a>, and a younger brother, also named <a href="/wiki/Magnus_von_Braun" title="Magnus von Braun">Magnus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mag8_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-mag8-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>After Wernher von Braun's <a href="/wiki/Confirmation_(Lutheran_Church)" title="Confirmation (Lutheran Church)">Lutheran confirmation</a>, his mother gave him a <a href="/wiki/Telescope" title="Telescope">telescope</a>, and he developed a passion for <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a>. The family moved to Berlin in 1915 where his father worked at the Ministry of the Interior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Here 12-year-old Wernher von Braun, inspired by speed records established by <a href="/wiki/Max_Valier" title="Max Valier">Max Valier</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fritz_von_Opel" title="Fritz von Opel">Fritz von Opel</a> in rocket-propelled cars,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-recoll_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-recoll-10">[10]</a></sup> caused a major disruption in a crowded street by detonating a toy wagon to which he had attached a number of fireworks. He was taken into custody by the local police until his father came to collect him.</p>
<p>Wernher von Braun was an accomplished amateur pianist who could play <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_van_Beethoven" title="Ludwig van Beethoven">Beethoven</a> and <a href="/wiki/Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="Johann Sebastian Bach">Bach</a> from memory. He learned to play both the cello and the piano at an early age and at one time wanted to become a composer. He took lessons from the composer <a href="/wiki/Paul_Hindemith" title="Paul Hindemith">Paul Hindemith</a>. The few pieces of von Braun’s youthful compositions that exist are reminiscent of Hindemith’s style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ward_2005_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ward_2005-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:11</sup></p>
<p>Beginning in 1925, von Braun attended a <a href="/wiki/Boarding_school" title="Boarding school">boarding school</a> at <a href="/wiki/Ettersburg" title="Ettersburg">Ettersburg Castle</a> near <a href="/wiki/Weimar" title="Weimar">Weimar</a>, where he did not do well in physics and mathematics. There he acquired a copy of <i>By Rocket into Planetary Space</i> (<i>Die Rakete zu den Planetenräumen</i>, 1923)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> by rocket pioneer <a href="/wiki/Hermann_Oberth" title="Hermann Oberth">Hermann Oberth</a>. In 1928, his parents moved him to the Hermann-Lietz-Internat (also a residential school) on the <a href="/wiki/East_Frisia" title="East Frisia">East Frisian</a> <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a> island of <a href="/wiki/Spiekeroog" title="Spiekeroog">Spiekeroog</a>. Space travel had always fascinated von Braun, and from then on he applied himself to <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a> to pursue his interest in rocket engineering.</p>
<p>In 1930, he attended the <a href="/wiki/Technical_University_of_Berlin" title="Technical University of Berlin">Technische Hochschule Berlin</a>, where he joined the <i>Spaceflight Society</i> (<a href="/wiki/Verein_f%C3%BCr_Raumschiffahrt" title="Verein für Raumschiffahrt">Verein für Raumschiffahrt</a> or "VfR") and assisted <a href="/wiki/Willy_Ley" title="Willy Ley">Willy Ley</a> in his liquid-fueled rocket motor tests in conjunction with <a href="/wiki/Hermann_Oberth" title="Hermann Oberth">Hermann Oberth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> In spring 1932, he graduated from the <i><a href="/wiki/Technische_Hochschule" title="Technische Hochschule">Technische Hochschule</a> Berlin</i> (now <a href="/wiki/Technical_University_of_Berlin" title="Technical University of Berlin">Technical University of Berlin</a>), with a <a href="/wiki/Diplom" title="Diplom">diploma</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_engineering" title="Mechanical engineering">mechanical engineering</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biography.com_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-biography.com-14">[14]</a></sup> His early exposure to rocketry convinced him that the exploration of space would require far more than applications of the current engineering technology. Wanting to learn more about <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a>, von Braun entered the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_Berlin" title="University of Berlin">Friedrich-Wilhelm University of Berlin</a> for post-graduate studies and graduated with a <a href="/wiki/Doctorate" title="Doctorate">doctorate</a> in physics in 1934.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history.msfc.nasa.gov_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-history.msfc.nasa.gov-15">[15]</a></sup> He also studied at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ETH_Z%C3%BCrich" title="ETH Zürich">ETH Zürich</a>. Although he worked mainly on military rockets in his later years there, space travel remained his primary interest.</p>
<p>In 1930, von Braun attended a presentation given by <a href="/wiki/Auguste_Piccard" title="Auguste Piccard">Auguste Piccard</a>. After the talk the young student approached the famous pioneer of high-altitude balloon flight, and stated to him: "You know, I plan on traveling to the Moon at some time." Piccard is said to have responded with encouraging words.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>He was greatly influenced by Oberth, of whom he said:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Hermann Oberth was the first, who when thinking about the possibility of spaceships grabbed a slide-rule and presented mathematically analyzed concepts and designs... I, myself, owe to him not only the guiding-star of my life, but also my first contact with the theoretical and practical aspects of rocketry and space travel. A place of honor should be reserved in the history of science and technology for his ground-breaking contributions in the field of astronautics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="German_career">German career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wernher_von_Braun&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: German career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Involvement_with_the_Nazi_regime">Involvement with the Nazi regime</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wernher_von_Braun&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Involvement with the Nazi regime">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Party_membership">Party membership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wernher_von_Braun&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Party membership">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Von Braun had an ambivalent and complex relationship with the Nazi regime of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Third_Reich" title="Third Reich">Third Reich</a>. He officially applied for membership in the <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Party" title="Nazi Party">Nazi Party</a> on November 12, 1937 and was issued with membership number 5,738,692.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neufeld_2007_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Neufeld_2007-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:96</sup></p>
<p>Michael J. Neufeld, the widely published author of aerospace history and the chief of the Space History Division at the Smithsonian's <a href="/wiki/National_Air_and_Space_Museum" title="National Air and Space Museum">National Air and Space Museum</a>, wrote that ten years after von Braun obtained his Nazi Party membership, he produced an affidavit for the U.S. Army misrepresenting the year of his membership, saying incorrectly:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neufeld_2007_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Neufeld_2007-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:96</sup></p>
<blockquote>
<p>In 1939, I was officially demanded to join the National Socialist Party. At this time I was already Technical Director at the Army Rocket Center at Peenemünde (Baltic Sea). The technical work carried out there had, in the meantime, attracted more and more attention in higher levels. Thus, my refusal to join the party would have meant that I would have to abandon the work of my life. Therefore, I decided to join. My membership in the party did not involve any political activity.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Whether von Braun's error with regard to the year was deliberate or a simple mistake has never been ascertained, although Neufeld stated that he might have lied on the affidavit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neufeld_2007_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-Neufeld_2007-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:96</sup> Neufeld further wrote:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>Von Braun, like other Peenemünders, was assigned to the local group in Karlshagen; there is no evidence that he did more than send in his monthly dues. But he is seen in some photographs with the party's swastika pin in his lapel – it was politically useful to demonstrate his membership.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neufeld_2007_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-Neufeld_2007-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:96</sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>His attitude toward the National Socialist regime in the late 1930s and early 1940s is difficult to understand. By his own account, he had been so influenced by the early Nazi promise of release from the <a href="/wiki/Post%E2%80%93World_War_I_recession" title="Post–World War I recession">post–World War I economic effects</a>, that his <a href="/wiki/Patriotism" title="Patriotism">patriotic</a> feelings had increased.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In a 1952 memoir article he admitted that, at that time, he "fared relatively rather well under <a href="/wiki/Totalitarianism" title="Totalitarianism">totalitarianism</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neufeld_2007_18-4"><a href="#cite_note-Neufeld_2007-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:96–97</sup> Yet, he also wrote that "to us, Hitler was still only a pompous fool with a <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Chaplin" title="Charlie Chaplin">Charlie Chaplin</a> moustache"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> and that he perceived him as "another <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon</a>" who was "wholly without scruples, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Godlessness" title="Godlessness">godless</a> man who thought himself the only god".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Membership_in_the_Allgemeine_SS">Membership in the Allgemeine SS</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wernher_von_Braun&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Membership in the Allgemeine SS">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Von Braun joined the SS horseback riding school on 1 November 1933 as an <i>SS-Anwärter</i>. He left the following year.<sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:63</sup> In 1940, he joined the SS<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ward_2009_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ward_2009-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:47</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> and was given the rank of "Untersturmfuehrer" (lieutenant) in the <a href="/wiki/Allgemeine_SS" title="Allgemeine SS">Allgemeine SS</a> and issued with membership number 185,068.<sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:121</sup> In 1947, he gave the U.S. War Department this explanation:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>In spring 1940, one SS-Standartenfuehrer (SS-colonel) Mueller from Greifswald, a bigger town in the vicinity of Peenemünde, looked me up in my office ... and told me, that Reichsfuehrer SS Himmler had sent him with the order to urge me to join the SS. I told him I was so busy with my rocket work that I had no time to spare for any political activity. He then told me, that ... the SS would cost me no time at all. I would be awarded the rank of a[n] "Untersturmfuehrer" (lieutenant) and it were  [<i><a href="/wiki/Sic" title="Sic">sic</a></i>] a very definite desire of Himmler that I attend his invitation to join.</p>
<p>I asked Mueller to give me some time for reflection. He agreed.</p>
<p>Realizing that the matter was of highly political significance for the relation between the SS and the Army, I called immediately on my military superior ..., Dr. Dornberger. He informed me that the SS had for a long time been trying to get their "finger in the pie" of the rocket work. I asked him what to do. He replied on the spot that if I wanted to continue our mutual work, I had no alternative but to join.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>When shown a picture of himself standing behind Himmler, von Braun claimed to have worn the SS uniform only that one time,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> but in 2002 a former SS officer at Peenemünde told the BBC that von Braun had regularly worn the SS uniform to official meetings. He began as an <a href="/wiki/Untersturmf%C3%BChrer" title="Untersturmführer">Untersturmführer</a> (Second lieutenant) and was promoted three times by Himmler, the last time in June 1943 to SS-<a href="/wiki/Sturmbannf%C3%BChrer" title="Sturmbannführer">Sturmbannführer</a> (Major). Von Braun later claimed that these were simply technical promotions received each year regularly by mail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dr._Space_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dr._Space-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>Von Braun's feelings for the Nazi regime may have changed<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> when he was arrested and accused of being a "communist sympathizer" who had attempted to sabotage or delay the weapons program.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> These charges could have resulted in the death penalty for treason. Not helping the matter was that von Braun had skills as a pilot and had access to an aircraft, which might have allowed him to escape to England.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Working_with_the_Nazis">Working with the Nazis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wernher_von_Braun&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Working with the Nazis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1978-Anh.024-03,_Peenem%C3%BCnde,_Dornberger,_Olbricht,_Brandt,_v._Braun.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="568" data-file-width="800" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1978-Anh.024-03%2C_Peenem%C3%BCnde%2C_Dornberger%2C_Olbricht%2C_Brandt%2C_v._Braun.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1978-Anh.024-03%2C_Peenem%C3%BCnde%2C_Dornberger%2C_Olbricht%2C_Brandt%2C_v._Braun.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1978-Anh.024-03%2C_Peenem%C3%BCnde%2C_Dornberger%2C_Olbricht%2C_Brandt%2C_v._Braun.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1978-Anh.024-03%2C_Peenem%C3%BCnde%2C_Dornberger%2C_Olbricht%2C_Brandt%2C_v._Braun.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1978-Anh.024-03%2C_Peenem%C3%BCnde%2C_Dornberger%2C_Olbricht%2C_Brandt%2C_v._Braun.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1978-Anh.024-03%2C_Peenem%C3%BCnde%2C_Dornberger%2C_Olbricht%2C_Brandt%2C_v._Braun.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1978-Anh.024-03,_Peenem%C3%BCnde,_Dornberger,_Olbricht,_Brandt,_v._Braun.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
First rank, from left to right, General Dr <a href="/wiki/Walter_Dornberger" title="Walter Dornberger">Walter Dornberger</a> (partially hidden), General <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Olbricht" title="Friedrich Olbricht">Friedrich Olbricht</a> (with Knight's Cross), Major Heinz Brandt, and Wernher von Braun (in civil garment) at <a href="/wiki/Peenem%C3%BCnde" title="Peenemünde">Peenemünde</a>, in March 1941.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1933, von Braun was working on his creative doctorate when the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Socialist_German_Workers_Party" title="National Socialist German Workers Party">National Socialist German Workers Party</a> (NSDAP, or Nazi Party) came to power in a coalition government in Germany; rocketry almost immediately moved onto the national agenda. An artillery captain, <a href="/wiki/Walter_Dornberger" title="Walter Dornberger">Walter Dornberger</a>, arranged an <a href="/wiki/Military_logistics" title="Military logistics">Ordnance</a> Department research grant for von Braun, who then worked next to Dornberger's existing solid-fuel rocket test site at <a href="/wiki/Kummersdorf" title="Kummersdorf">Kummersdorf</a>.</p>
<p>He was awarded a doctorate in physics<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astronautix_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-astronautix-25">[25]</a></sup> (<a href="/wiki/Aerospace_engineering" title="Aerospace engineering">aerospace engineering</a>) on July 27, 1934, from the <a href="/wiki/Humboldt_University_of_Berlin" title="Humboldt University of Berlin">University of Berlin</a> for a thesis entitled "<i>About Combustion Tests"</i>; his doctoral supervisor was <a href="/wiki/Erich_Schumann" title="Erich Schumann">Erich Schumann</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neufeld_2007_18-5"><a href="#cite_note-Neufeld_2007-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:61</sup> However, this thesis was only the public part of von Braun's work. His actual full thesis, <i>Construction, Theoretical, and Experimental Solution to the Problem of the Liquid Propellant Rocket</i> (dated April 16, 1934) was kept classified by the army,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (January 2017)">which?</span></a></i>]</sup> and was not published until 1960.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> By the end of 1934, his group had successfully launched two liquid fuel rockets that rose to heights of 2.2 and 3.5 km (2 mi).</p>
<p>At the time, Germany was highly interested in American physicist <a href="/wiki/Robert_H._Goddard" title="Robert H. Goddard">Robert H. Goddard</a>'s research. Before 1939, German scientists occasionally contacted Goddard directly with technical questions. Wernher von Braun used Goddard's plans from various journals and incorporated them into the building of the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aggregate_series" title="Aggregate series">Aggregat</a></i> (A) series of <a href="/wiki/Rocket" title="Rocket">rockets</a>. The A-4 rocket would become well known as the V-2.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> In 1963, von Braun reflected on the history of rocketry, and said of Goddard's work: "His rockets ... may have been rather crude by present-day standards, but they blazed the trail and incorporated many features used in our most modern rockets and space vehicles."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-recoll_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-recoll-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>Goddard confirmed his work was used by von Braun in 1944, shortly before the Nazis began firing V-2s at England. A V-2 crashed in Sweden and some parts were sent to an Annapolis lab where Goddard was doing research for the Navy. If this was the so-called <a href="/wiki/Sweden_during_World_War_II#The_B.C3.A4ckebo_rocket" title="Sweden during World War II">Bäckebo Bomb</a>, it had been procured by the British in exchange for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spitfire" title="Spitfire">Spitfires</a>; Annapolis would have received some parts from them. Goddard is reported to have recognized components he had invented, and inferred that his brainchild had been turned into a weapon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Later, von Braun would comment: “I have very deep and sincere regret for the victims of the V-2 rockets, but there were victims on both sides... A war is a war, and when my country is at war, my duty is to help win that war.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>There were no German rocket societies after the collapse of the <a href="/wiki/Verein_f%C3%BCr_Raumschiffahrt" title="Verein für Raumschiffahrt">VfR</a>, and civilian rocket tests were forbidden by the new <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi regime</a>. Only military development was allowed and to this end, a larger facility was erected at the village of <a href="/wiki/Peenem%C3%BCnde" title="Peenemünde">Peenemünde</a> in northern Germany on the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a>. Dornberger became the military commander at Peenemünde, with von Braun as technical director. In collaboration with the <a href="/wiki/Luftwaffe" title="Luftwaffe">Luftwaffe</a>, the Peenemünde group developed liquid-fuel rocket engines for aircraft and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jet-assisted_takeoff" title="Jet-assisted takeoff">jet-assisted takeoffs</a>. They also developed the long-range <a href="/wiki/V-2_rocket" title="V-2 rocket">A-4</a> <a href="/wiki/Ballistic_missile" title="Ballistic missile">ballistic missile</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supersonic" title="Supersonic">supersonic</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wasserfall_missile" title="Wasserfall missile">Wasserfall</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-aircraft_missile" title="Anti-aircraft missile">anti-aircraft missile</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:V-2_rocket_diagram_(with_English_labels).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="654" data-file-width="490" height="294" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/V-2_rocket_diagram_%28with_English_labels%29.svg/220px-V-2_rocket_diagram_%28with_English_labels%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/V-2_rocket_diagram_%28with_English_labels%29.svg/330px-V-2_rocket_diagram_%28with_English_labels%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/V-2_rocket_diagram_%28with_English_labels%29.svg/440px-V-2_rocket_diagram_%28with_English_labels%29.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:V-2_rocket_diagram_(with_English_labels).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Schematic of the A4/V2</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On December 22, 1942, <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> signed the order approving the production of the A-4 as a "vengeance weapon" and the group developed it to target London. Following von Braun's July 7, 1943 presentation of a color movie showing an A-4 taking off, Hitler was so enthusiastic that he personally made von Braun a professor shortly thereafter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> In Germany at this time, this was an exceptional promotion for an engineer who was only 31 years old.</p>
<p>By that time, the British and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soviet_intelligence" title="Soviet intelligence">Soviet intelligence</a> agencies were aware of the rocket program and von Braun's team at Peenemünde, based on the intelligence provided by the Polish underground <a href="/wiki/Home_Army" title="Home Army">Home Army</a>. Over the nights of August 17–18, 1943, <a href="/wiki/RAF_Bomber_Command" title="RAF Bomber Command">RAF Bomber Command</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Operation_Hydra_(1943)" title="Operation Hydra (1943)">Operation Hydra</a> dispatched raids on the Peenemünde camp consisting of 596 aircraft, and dropped 1,800 tons of explosives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RAF_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-RAF-31">[31]</a></sup> The facility was salvaged and most of the engineering team remained unharmed; however, the raids killed von Braun's engine designer <a href="/wiki/Walter_Thiel" title="Walter Thiel">Walter Thiel</a> and Chief Engineer Walther, and the rocket program was delayed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dornberger_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dornberger-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Peenem%C3%BCnde_in_World_War_II" title="Bombing of Peenemünde in World War II">Bombing of Peenemünde in World War II</a></div>
<p>The first combat A-4, renamed the <a href="/wiki/V-2_rocket" title="V-2 rocket">V-2</a> (<i>Vergeltungswaffe 2</i> "Retaliation/Vengeance Weapon 2") for propaganda purposes, was launched toward England on September 7, 1944, only 21 months after the project had been officially commissioned. Von Braun's interest in rockets was specifically for the application of <a href="/wiki/Spacefaring" title="Spacefaring">space travel</a>, not for killing people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> After hearing the news from London, he said that "the rocket worked perfectly, except for landing on the wrong planet." Satirist <a href="/wiki/Mort_Sahl" title="Mort Sahl">Mort Sahl</a> is often credited with mocking von Braun with the paraphrase "<i>I aim at the stars, but sometimes I hit London</i>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sahl_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-sahl-35">[35]</a></sup> In fact that line appears in the film <i><a href="/wiki/I_Aim_at_the_Stars" title="I Aim at the Stars">I Aim at the Stars</a></i>, a 1960 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biopic" title="Biopic">biopic</a> of von Braun.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Experiments_with_rocket_aircraft">Experiments with rocket aircraft</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wernher_von_Braun&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Experiments with rocket aircraft">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During 1936, von Braun's rocketry team working at Kummersdorf investigated installing liquid-fuelled rockets in aircraft. <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Heinkel" title="Ernst Heinkel">Ernst Heinkel</a> enthusiastically supported their efforts, supplying a <a href="/wiki/Heinkel_He_72" title="Heinkel He 72">He-72</a> and later two <a href="/wiki/Heinkel_He_112" title="Heinkel He 112">He-112s</a> for the experiments. Late in 1936, <a href="/wiki/Erich_Warsitz" title="Erich Warsitz">Erich Warsitz</a> was seconded by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reich_Air_Ministry" title="Reich Air Ministry">RLM</a> to Wernher von Braun and Ernst Heinkel, because he had been recognized as one of the most experienced test-pilots of the time, and because he also had an extraordinary fund of technical knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-warsitz_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-warsitz-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:30</sup> After he familiarized Warsitz with a test-stand run, showing him the corresponding apparatus in the aircraft, he asked: "Are you with us and will you test the rocket in the air? Then, Warsitz, you will be a famous man. And later we will fly to the Moon – with you at the helm!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-warsitz_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-warsitz-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:35</sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:He112FARR.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="272" data-file-width="567" height="106" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/He112FARR.jpg/220px-He112FARR.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/He112FARR.jpg/330px-He112FARR.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/He112FARR.jpg/440px-He112FARR.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:He112FARR.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A regular He 112</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In June 1937, at <a href="/wiki/Neuhardenberg" title="Neuhardenberg">Neuhardenberg</a> (a large field about 70 km (43 mi) east of Berlin, listed as a reserve airfield in the event of war), one of these latter aircraft was flown with its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Piston_engine" title="Piston engine">piston engine</a> shut down during flight by test pilot Erich Warsitz, at which time it was propelled by von Braun’s rocket power alone. Despite the wheels-up landing and having the fuselage on fire, it proved to official circles that an aircraft could be flown satisfactorily with a back-thrust system through the rear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-warsitz_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-warsitz-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:51</sup></p>
<p>At the same time, <a href="/wiki/Hellmuth_Walter" title="Hellmuth Walter">Hellmuth Walter</a>'s experiments into <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_peroxide" title="Hydrogen peroxide">hydrogen peroxide</a> based rockets were leading towards light and simple rockets that appeared well-suited for aircraft installation. Also the firm of Hellmuth Walter at Kiel had been commissioned by the RLM to build a rocket engine for the <a href="/wiki/Heinkel_He_112" title="Heinkel He 112">He 112</a>, so there were two different new rocket motor designs at Neuhardenberg: whereas von Braun’s engines were powered by alcohol and liquid oxygen, Walter engines had hydrogen peroxide and <a href="/wiki/Calcium_permanganate" title="Calcium permanganate">calcium permanganate</a> as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalyst" title="Catalyst">catalyst</a>. Von Braun’s engines used direct combustion and created fire, the Walter devices used hot vapors from a chemical reaction, but both created thrust and provided high speed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-warsitz_36-3"><a href="#cite_note-warsitz-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:41</sup> The subsequent flights with the He-112 used the Walter-rocket instead of von Braun's; it was more reliable, simpler to operate, and safer for test pilot Erich Warsitz.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-warsitz_36-4"><a href="#cite_note-warsitz-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:55</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Slave_labor">Slave labor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wernher_von_Braun&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Slave labor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>SS General <a href="/wiki/Hans_Kammler" title="Hans Kammler">Hans Kammler</a>, who as an engineer had constructed several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Concentration_camp" title="Concentration camp">concentration camps</a>, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Auschwitz" title="Auschwitz">Auschwitz</a>, had a reputation for brutality and had originated the idea of using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Forced_labor_in_Germany_during_World_War_II" title="Forced labor in Germany during World War II">concentration camp prisoners as slave laborers</a> in the rocket program. <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Rudolph" title="Arthur Rudolph">Arthur Rudolph</a>, chief engineer of the V-2 rocket factory at Peenemünde, endorsed this idea in April 1943 when a labor shortage developed. More people died building the V-2 rockets than were killed by it as a weapon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Von Braun admitted visiting the plant at <a href="/wiki/Mittelwerk" title="Mittelwerk">Mittelwerk</a> on many occasions, and called conditions at the plant "repulsive", but claimed never to have witnessed any deaths or beatings, although it had become clear to him by 1944 that deaths had occurred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-power2_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-power2-38">[38]</a></sup> He denied ever having visited the <a href="/wiki/Mittelbau-Dora" title="Mittelbau-Dora">Mittelbau-Dora</a> concentration camp itself, where 20,000 died from illness, beatings, hangings, and intolerable working conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TimeD_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-TimeD-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some prisoners claim von Braun engaged in brutal treatment or approved of it. Guy Morand, a French resistance fighter who was a prisoner in Dora, testified in 1995 that after an apparent sabotage attempt that von Braun ordered a prisoner to be flogged,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bid_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-bid-40">[40]</a></sup> while Robert Cazabonne, another French prisoner, claimed von Braun stood by as prisoners were hanged by chains suspended by cranes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bid_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-bid-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:123–124</sup> However, these accounts may have been a case of mistaken identity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Former <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buchenwald" title="Buchenwald">Buchenwald</a> inmate Adam Cabala claims that von Braun went to the concentration camp to pick slave laborers: "[...] also the German scientists led by Prof. Wernher von Braun were aware of everything daily. As they went along the corridors, they saw the exhaustion of the inmates, their arduous work and their pain. Not one single time did Prof. Wernher von Braun protest against this cruelty and bestiality during his frequent stays at Dora. Even the aspect of corpses did not touch him: On a small area near the ambulance shed, inmates tortured to death by slave labor and the terror of the overseers were piling up daily. But, Prof. Wernher von Braun passed them so close that he was almost touching the corpses".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <i>Wernher von Braun: Crusader for Space</i>, numerous statements by von Braun show he was aware of the conditions but felt completely unable to change them. A friend quotes von Braun speaking of a visit to Mittelwerk:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>It is hellish. My spontaneous reaction was to talk to one of the SS guards, only to be told with unmistakable harshness that I should mind my own business, or find myself in the same striped fatigues! ... I realized that any attempt of reasoning on humane grounds would be utterly futile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StuhlingerOrdway1994_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-StuhlingerOrdway1994-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>When asked if von Braun could have protested against the brutal treatment of the slave laborers, von Braun team member <a href="/wiki/Konrad_Dannenberg" title="Konrad Dannenberg">Konrad Dannenberg</a> told <i>The Huntsville Times</i>, "If he had done it, in my opinion, he would have been shot on the spot."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arrest_and_release_by_the_Nazi_regime">Arrest and release by the Nazi regime</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wernher_von_Braun&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Arrest and release by the Nazi regime">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to André Sellier, a French historian and survivor of the <a href="/wiki/Mittelbau-Dora" title="Mittelbau-Dora">Mittelbau-Dora</a> concentration camp, <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Himmler" title="Heinrich Himmler">Heinrich Himmler</a> had von Braun come to his Feldkommandostelle Hochwald HQ in <a href="/wiki/East_Prussia" title="East Prussia">East Prussia</a> in February 1944.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sellier_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-sellier-45">[45]</a></sup> To increase his power-base within the Nazi regime, Himmler was conspiring to use Kammler to gain control of all German armament programs, including the V-2 program at Peenemünde.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ward_2005_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ward_2005-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:38–40</sup> He therefore recommended that von Braun work more closely with Kammler to solve the problems of the V-2. Von Braun claimed to have replied that the problems were merely technical and he was confident that they would be solved with Dornberger's assistance.</p>
<p>Von Braun had been under <a href="/wiki/Sicherheitsdienst" title="Sicherheitsdienst">SD</a> surveillance since October 1943. A report stated that he and his colleagues <a href="/wiki/Klaus_Riedel" title="Klaus Riedel">Riedel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Helmut_Gr%C3%B6ttrup" title="Helmut Gröttrup">Gröttrup</a> were said to have expressed regret at an engineer's house one evening that they were not working on a spaceship and that they felt the war was not going well; this was considered a "defeatist" attitude. A young female dentist who was an SS spy reported their comments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ward_2005_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ward_2005-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:38–40</sup> Combined with Himmler's false charges that von Braun was a communist sympathizer and had attempted to sabotage the V-2 program, and considering that von Braun regularly piloted his government-provided airplane that might allow him to escape to England, this led to his arrest by the <a href="/wiki/Gestapo" title="Gestapo">Gestapo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ward_2005_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-Ward_2005-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:38–40</sup></p>
<p>The unsuspecting von Braun was detained on March 14 (or March 15),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grdev_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-grdev-46">[46]</a></sup> 1944, and was taken to a Gestapo cell in <a href="/wiki/Szczecin" title="Szczecin">Stettin</a> (now Szczecin, Poland),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ward_2005_11-4"><a href="#cite_note-Ward_2005-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:38–40</sup> where he was held for two weeks without knowing the charges against him.</p>
<p>Through the <a href="/wiki/Abwehr" title="Abwehr">Abwehr</a> in Berlin, Dornberger obtained von Braun's conditional release and <a href="/wiki/Albert_Speer" title="Albert Speer">Albert Speer</a>, Reichsminister for Munitions and War Production, convinced Hitler to reinstate von Braun so that the V-2 program could continue<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ward_2005_11-5"><a href="#cite_note-Ward_2005-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:38–40</sup> or turn into a "V-4 program" which in their view would be impossible without von Braun's leadership.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Quoting from the "Führerprotokoll" (the minutes of Hitler's meetings) dated May 13, 1944, in his memoirs, Speer later relayed what Hitler had finally conceded: "In the matter concerning B. I will guarantee you that he will be exempt from prosecution as long as he is indispensable for you, in spite of the difficult general consequences this will have."</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dornberger-Axter-von_Braun.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3072" data-file-width="2048" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Dornberger-Axter-von_Braun.jpg/220px-Dornberger-Axter-von_Braun.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Dornberger-Axter-von_Braun.jpg/330px-Dornberger-Axter-von_Braun.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Dornberger-Axter-von_Braun.jpg/440px-Dornberger-Axter-von_Braun.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dornberger-Axter-von_Braun.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Von Braun, with his arm in a cast from a car accident, <a href="/wiki/Surrender_(military)" title="Surrender (military)">surrendered</a> to the Americans just before this May 3, 1945 photo.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Surrender_to_the_Americans">Surrender to the Americans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wernher_von_Braun&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Surrender to the Americans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Army" title="Soviet Army">Soviet Army</a> was about 160 km (99 mi) from <a href="/wiki/Peenem%C3%BCnde" title="Peenemünde">Peenemünde</a> in the spring of 1945 when von Braun assembled his planning staff and asked them to decide how and to whom they should surrender. Afraid of the well-known Soviet cruelty to prisoners of war<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>, von Braun and his staff decided to try to surrender to the Americans. Kammler had ordered relocation of his team to central Germany; however, a conflicting order from an army chief ordered them to join the army and fight. Deciding that Kammler's order was their best bet to defect to the Americans, von Braun fabricated documents and transported 500 of his affiliates to the area around Mittelwerk, where they resumed their work. For fear of their documents being destroyed by the SS, von Braun ordered the blueprints to be hidden in an abandoned mine shaft in the <a href="/wiki/Harz" title="Harz">Harz</a> mountain range.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cadbury_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cadbury-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>While on an official trip in March, von Braun suffered a complicated fracture of his left arm and shoulder in a car accident after his driver fell asleep at the wheel. His injuries were serious, but he insisted that his arm be set in a cast so he could leave the hospital. Due to this neglect of the injury he had to be hospitalized again a month later where his bones had to be re-broken and re-aligned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cadbury_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cadbury-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>In April, as the Allied forces advanced deeper into Germany, Kammler ordered the engineering team to be moved by train into the town of <a href="/wiki/Oberammergau" title="Oberammergau">Oberammergau</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Bavarian_Alps" title="Bavarian Alps">Bavarian Alps</a> where they were closely guarded by the SS with orders to execute the team if they were about to fall into enemy hands. However, von Braun managed to convince SS Major Kummer to order the dispersion of the group into nearby villages so that they would not be an easy target for U.S. bombers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cadbury_48-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cadbury-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>Von Braun and a large number of the engineering team subsequently made it to Austria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> On May 2, 1945, upon finding an American private from the U.S. <a href="/wiki/44th_Infantry_Division_(United_States)" title="44th Infantry Division (United States)">44th Infantry Division</a>, von Braun's brother and fellow rocket engineer, Magnus, approached the soldier on a bicycle, calling out in broken English: "My name is Magnus von Braun. My brother invented the V-2. We want to surrender."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mag8_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-mag8-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> After the surrender, Wernher spoke to the press:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>We knew that we had created a new means of warfare, and the question as to what nation, to what victorious nation we were willing to entrust this brainchild of ours was a moral decision more than anything else. We wanted to see the world spared another conflict such as Germany had just been through, and we felt that only by surrendering such a weapon to people who are guided by the Bible could such an assurance to the world be best secured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>The American high command was well aware of how important their catch was: von Braun had been at the top of <i>the Black List</i>, the code name for the list of German scientists and engineers targeted for immediate interrogation by U.S. military experts. On June 19, 1945, two days before the scheduled handover of the <a href="/wiki/Nordhausen" title="Nordhausen">Nordhausen</a> area to the Soviets, U.S. Army Major Robert B. Staver, Chief of the Jet Propulsion Section of the Research and Intelligence Branch of the <a href="/wiki/Ordnance_Corps_(United_States_Army)" title="Ordnance Corps (United States Army)">U.S. Army Ordnance Corps</a> in London, and Lt Col R. L. Williams took von Braun and his department chiefs by Jeep from Garmisch to Munich and then flown to Nordhausen; on the next day the group was evacuated 40 miles (64 km) southwest to <a href="/wiki/Witzenhausen" title="Witzenhausen">Witzenhausen</a>, a small town in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_Zone" title="American Zone">American Zone</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McGovern_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-McGovern-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>Von Braun was briefly detained at the "Dustbin" interrogation center at <a href="/wiki/Kransberg_Castle" title="Kransberg Castle">Kransberg Castle</a> where the elite of the Third Reich's economy, science and technology were debriefed by U.S. and British intelligence officials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Initially he was recruited to the U.S. under a program called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Operation_Overcast" title="Operation Overcast">Operation Overcast</a>, subsequently known as <a href="/wiki/Operation_Paperclip" title="Operation Paperclip">Operation Paperclip</a>. There is evidence, however, that British intelligence and scientists were the first to interview him in depth, eager to gain information that they knew U.S. officials would deny them. The team included the young L.S. Snell, then the leading British rocket engineer, later chief designer of <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Limited" title="Rolls-Royce Limited">Rolls-Royce Limited</a> and inventor of the <a href="/wiki/Concorde" title="Concorde">Concorde</a>'s engines. The specific information the British gleaned remained top secret, both from the Americans and other allies.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="American_career">American career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wernher_von_Braun&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: American career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="U.S._Army_career">U.S. Army career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wernher_von_Braun&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: U.S. Army career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NACA%27s_Special_Committee_on_Space_Technology.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2400" data-file-width="3000" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/NACA%27s_Special_Committee_on_Space_Technology.jpg/220px-NACA%27s_Special_Committee_on_Space_Technology.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/NACA%27s_Special_Committee_on_Space_Technology.jpg/330px-NACA%27s_Special_Committee_on_Space_Technology.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/NACA%27s_Special_Committee_on_Space_Technology.jpg/440px-NACA%27s_Special_Committee_on_Space_Technology.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NACA%27s_Special_Committee_on_Space_Technology.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Wernher von Braun at a meeting of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NACA" title="NACA">NACA</a>'s Special Committee on Space Technology, 1958</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On June 20, 1945, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">U.S. Secretary of State</a> approved the transfer of von Braun and his specialists to America; however, this was not announced to the public until October 1, 1945.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-paper_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-paper-54">[54]</a></sup> Von Braun was among those scientists for whom the U.S. Joint Intelligence Objectives Agency created false employment histories and expunged Nazi Party memberships and regime affiliations from the public record. Once “bleached” of their Nazism, the U.S. Government granted the scientists security clearance to work in the United States.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The first seven technicians arrived in the United States at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Castle_Army_Air_Field" title="New Castle Army Air Field">New Castle Army Air Field</a>, just south of <a href="/wiki/Wilmington,_Delaware" title="Wilmington, Delaware">Wilmington</a>, Delaware, on September 20, 1945. They were then flown to Boston and taken by boat to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Army_Military_Intelligence" title="United States Army Military Intelligence">Army Intelligence</a> Service post at <a href="/wiki/Fort_Strong" title="Fort Strong">Fort Strong</a> in <a href="/wiki/Boston_Harbor" title="Boston Harbor">Boston Harbor</a>. Later, with the exception of von Braun, the men were transferred to <a href="/wiki/Aberdeen_Proving_Ground" title="Aberdeen Proving Ground">Aberdeen Proving Ground</a> in <a href="/wiki/Maryland" title="Maryland">Maryland</a> to sort out the Peenemünde documents, enabling the scientists to continue their rocketry experiments.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Finally, von Braun and his remaining Peenemünde staff (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_German_rocket_scientists_in_the_United_States" title="List of German rocket scientists in the United States">List of German rocket scientists in the United States</a>) were transferred to their new home at <a href="/wiki/Fort_Bliss" title="Fort Bliss">Fort Bliss</a>, a large Army installation just north of <a href="/wiki/El_Paso,_Texas" title="El Paso, Texas">El Paso</a>. Von Braun would later write he found it hard to develop a "genuine emotional attachment" to his new surroundings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RM_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-RM-55">[55]</a></sup> His chief design engineer Walther Reidel became the subject of a December 1946 article "German Scientist Says American Cooking Tasteless; Dislikes Rubberized Chicken", exposing the presence of von Braun's team in the country and drawing criticism from <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_D._Dingell,_Sr." title="John D. Dingell, Sr.">John Dingell</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RM_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-RM-55">[55]</a></sup> Requests to improve their living conditions such as laying linoleum over their cracked wood flooring were rejected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RM_55-2"><a href="#cite_note-RM-55">[55]</a></sup> Von Braun remarked, "...at Peenemünde we had been coddled, here you were counting pennies..."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RM_55-3"><a href="#cite_note-RM-55">[55]</a></sup> At Peenemünde, von Braun had thousands of engineers who answered to him, but was now answering to "pimply" 26-year-old Major Jim Hamill who possessed an undergraduate degree in engineering.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RM_55-4"><a href="#cite_note-RM-55">[55]</a></sup> His loyal Germans still addressed him as Herr Professor, but Hamill addressed him as Wernher and never bothered to respond to von Braun's request for more materials, and every proposal for new rocket ideas was dismissed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RM_55-5"><a href="#cite_note-RM-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wernher_von_Braun_-_ABMA_Badge.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="920" data-file-width="642" height="315" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Wernher_von_Braun_-_ABMA_Badge.jpg/220px-Wernher_von_Braun_-_ABMA_Badge.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Wernher_von_Braun_-_ABMA_Badge.jpg/330px-Wernher_von_Braun_-_ABMA_Badge.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Wernher_von_Braun_-_ABMA_Badge.jpg/440px-Wernher_von_Braun_-_ABMA_Badge.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wernher_von_Braun_-_ABMA_Badge.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Von Braun's badge at ABMA (1957)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>While there, they trained military, industrial, and university personnel in the intricacies of rockets and guided missiles. As part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hermes_project" title="Hermes project">Hermes project</a>, they helped refurbish, assemble, and launch a number of V-2s that had been shipped from Germany to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_Sands_Proving_Ground" title="White Sands Proving Ground">White Sands Proving Ground</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a>. They also continued to study the future potential of rockets for military and research applications. Since they were not permitted to leave Fort Bliss without military escort, von Braun and his colleagues began to refer to themselves only half-jokingly as "PoPs" – "Prisoners of Peace."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In 1950, at the start of the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a>, von Braun and his team were transferred to <a href="/wiki/Huntsville,_Alabama" title="Huntsville, Alabama">Huntsville, Alabama</a>, his home for the next 20 years. Between 1952 and 1956,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> von Braun led the Army's rocket development team at <a href="/wiki/Redstone_Arsenal" title="Redstone Arsenal">Redstone Arsenal</a>, resulting in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Redstone_(rocket)" title="Redstone (rocket)">Redstone rocket</a>, which was used for the first live <a href="/wiki/Ballistic_missile" title="Ballistic missile">nuclear ballistic missile</a> tests conducted by the United States. He personally witnessed this historic launch and detonation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Work on the Redstone led to development of the first high-precision inertial guidance system on the Redstone rocket.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NTRS_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-NTRS-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>As director of the Development Operations Division of the <a href="/wiki/Army_Ballistic_Missile_Agency" title="Army Ballistic Missile Agency">Army Ballistic Missile Agency</a>, von Braun, with his team, then developed the <a href="/wiki/Jupiter-C" title="Jupiter-C">Jupiter-C</a>, a modified Redstone rocket.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-time58_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-time58-59">[59]</a></sup> The Jupiter-C successfully launched the West's first satellite, <a href="/wiki/Explorer_1" title="Explorer 1">Explorer 1</a>, on January 31, 1958. This event signaled the birth of America's space program.</p>
<p>Despite the work on the Redstone rocket, the 12 years from 1945 to 1957 were probably some of the most frustrating for von Braun and his colleagues. In the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sergei_Korolev" title="Sergei Korolev">Sergei Korolev</a> and his team of scientists and engineers plowed ahead with several new rocket designs and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sputnik" title="Sputnik">Sputnik</a> program, while the American government was not very interested in von Braun's work or views and only embarked on a very modest rocket-building program. In the meantime, the press tended to dwell on von Braun's past as a member of the SS and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slave_labor" title="Slave labor">slave labor</a> used to build his V-2 rockets.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Popular_concepts_for_a_human_presence_in_space">Popular concepts for a human presence in space</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wernher_von_Braun&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Popular concepts for a human presence in space">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Repeating the pattern he had established during his earlier career in Germany, von Braun – while directing military rocket development in the real world – continued to entertain his engineer-scientist's dream of a future world in which rockets would be used for <a href="/wiki/Space_exploration" title="Space exploration">space exploration</a>. However, instead of risking being sacked, he now was increasingly in a position to popularize these ideas. The May 14, 1950, headline of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Huntsville_Times" title="The Huntsville Times">The Huntsville Times</a></i> ("Dr. von Braun Says Rocket Flights Possible to Moon") might have marked the beginning of these efforts. These disclosures rode a Moon flight publicity wave that was created by the two 1950 U.S. science fiction films, <i><a href="/wiki/Destination_Moon_(film)" title="Destination Moon (film)">Destination Moon</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Rocketship_X-M" title="Rocketship X-M">Rocketship X-M</a></i>.</p>
<p>In 1952, von Braun first published his concept of a manned <a href="/wiki/Space_station" title="Space station">space station</a> in a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Collier%27s_Weekly" title="Collier's Weekly">Collier's Weekly</a></i> magazine series of articles entitled "<a href="/wiki/Man_Will_Conquer_Space_Soon!" title="Man Will Conquer Space Soon!">Man Will Conquer Space Soon!</a>". These articles were illustrated by the space artist <a href="/wiki/Chesley_Bonestell" title="Chesley Bonestell">Chesley Bonestell</a> and were influential in spreading his ideas. Frequently, von Braun worked with fellow German-born space advocate and science writer <a href="/wiki/Willy_Ley" title="Willy Ley">Willy Ley</a> to publish his concepts, which, unsurprisingly, were heavy on the engineering side and anticipated many technical aspects of space flight that later became reality.</p>
<p>The space station (to be constructed using rockets with recoverable and reusable ascent stages) would be a <a href="/wiki/Stanford_torus" title="Stanford torus">toroid</a> structure, with a diameter of 250 feet (76 m); this built on the concept of a <a href="/wiki/Rotating_wheel_space_station" title="Rotating wheel space station">rotating wheel-shaped station</a> introduced in 1929 by <a href="/wiki/Herman_Poto%C4%8Dnik" title="Herman Potočnik">Herman Potočnik</a> in his book <i>The Problem of Space Travel – The Rocket Motor.</i> The space station would spin around a central docking nave to provide <a href="/wiki/Artificial_gravity" title="Artificial gravity">artificial gravity</a>, and would be assembled in a 1,075-mile (1,730 km) two-hour, high-inclination <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth_orbit" title="Earth orbit">Earth orbit</a> allowing observation of essentially every point on Earth on at least a daily basis. The ultimate purpose of the space station would be to provide an assembly platform for manned <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">lunar</a> expeditions. More than a decade later, the movie version of <a href="/wiki/2001:_A_Space_Odyssey_(film)" title="2001: A Space Odyssey (film)"><i>2001: A Space Odyssey</i></a> would draw heavily on the design concept in its visualization of an orbital space station.</p>
<p>Von Braun envisaged these expeditions as very large-scale undertakings, with a total of 50 astronauts travelling in three huge spacecraft (two for crew, one primarily for cargo), each 49 m (160.76 ft) long and 33 m (108.27 ft) in diameter and driven by a rectangular array of 30 rocket propulsion engines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seh1_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-seh1-60">[60]</a></sup> Upon arrival, astronauts would establish a <a href="/wiki/Colonization_of_the_Moon" title="Colonization of the Moon">permanent lunar base</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Sinus_Roris" title="Sinus Roris">Sinus Roris</a> region by using the emptied cargo holds of their craft as shelters, and would explore their surroundings for eight weeks. This would include a 400 km (249 mi) expedition in pressurized rovers to the crater <a href="/wiki/Harpalus_(crater)" title="Harpalus (crater)">Harpalus</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mare_Imbrium" title="Mare Imbrium">Mare Imbrium</a> foothills.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Walt_Disney_and_Dr._Wernher_von_Braun_-_GPN-2000-000060.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="2365" height="279" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Walt_Disney_and_Dr._Wernher_von_Braun_-_GPN-2000-000060.jpg/220px-Walt_Disney_and_Dr._Wernher_von_Braun_-_GPN-2000-000060.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Walt_Disney_and_Dr._Wernher_von_Braun_-_GPN-2000-000060.jpg/330px-Walt_Disney_and_Dr._Wernher_von_Braun_-_GPN-2000-000060.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Walt_Disney_and_Dr._Wernher_von_Braun_-_GPN-2000-000060.jpg/440px-Walt_Disney_and_Dr._Wernher_von_Braun_-_GPN-2000-000060.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Walt_Disney_and_Dr._Wernher_von_Braun_-_GPN-2000-000060.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney" title="Walt Disney">Walt Disney</a> and von Braun, seen in 1954 holding a model of his passenger ship, collaborated on a series of three educational films.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At this time, von Braun also worked out preliminary concepts for a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manned_mission_to_Mars" title="Manned mission to Mars">manned mission to Mars</a> that used the space station as a staging point. His initial plans, published in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Mars_Project" title="The Mars Project">The Mars Project</a></i> (1952), had envisaged a fleet of 10 spacecraft (each with a mass of 3,720 metric tons), three of them unmanned and each carrying one 200-ton winged lander<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seh1_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-seh1-60">[60]</a></sup> in addition to cargo, and nine crew vehicles transporting a total of 70 astronauts. Gigantic as this mission plan was, its engineering and astronautical parameters were thoroughly calculated. A later project was much more modest, using only one purely orbital cargo ship and one crewed craft. In each case, the expedition would use minimum-energy <a href="/wiki/Hohmann_transfer_orbit" title="Hohmann transfer orbit">Hohmann transfer orbits</a> for its trips to Mars and back to Earth.</p>
<p>Before technically formalizing his thoughts on human <a href="/wiki/Spaceflight" title="Spaceflight">spaceflight</a> to Mars, von Braun had written a science fiction novel on the subject, set in the year 1980. However, the manuscript was rejected by no fewer than 18 publishers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bergaust_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-bergaust-61">[61]</a></sup> Von Braun later published small portions of this opus in magazines, to illustrate selected aspects of his Mars project popularizations. The complete manuscript, titled <i>Project MARS: A Technical Tale</i>, did not appear as a printed book until December 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the hope that its involvement would bring about greater public interest in the future of the space program, von Braun also began working with <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney" title="Walt Disney">Walt Disney</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Pictures" title="Walt Disney Pictures">Disney studios</a> as a technical director, initially for three television films about space exploration. The initial broadcast devoted to space exploration was <i><a href="/wiki/Man_in_Space" title="Man in Space">Man in Space</a>,</i> which first went on air on March 9, 1955, drawing 40 million viewers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RM_55-6"><a href="#cite_note-RM-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ley195510_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-ley195510-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>Later (in 1959) von Braun published a short booklet, condensed from episodes that had appeared in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/This_Week_Magazine" title="This Week Magazine">This Week Magazine</a></i> before—describing his updated concept of the first manned lunar landing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> The scenario included only a single and relatively small spacecraft—a winged lander with a crew of only two experienced pilots who had already circumnavigated the Moon on an earlier mission. The brute-force <a href="/wiki/Direct_ascent" title="Direct ascent">direct ascent</a> flight schedule used a rocket design with five sequential stages, loosely based on the <a href="/wiki/Nova_(rocket)" title="Nova (rocket)">Nova</a> designs that were under discussion at this time. After a night launch from a Pacific island, the first three stages would bring the spacecraft (with the two remaining upper stages attached) to terrestrial <a href="/wiki/Escape_velocity" title="Escape velocity">escape velocity</a>, with each burn creating an acceleration of 8–9 times <a href="/wiki/Standard_gravity" title="Standard gravity">standard gravity</a>. Residual propellant in the third stage would be used for the deceleration intended to commence only a few hundred kilometers above the landing site in a crater near the lunar north pole. The fourth stage provided acceleration to lunar escape velocity, while the fifth stage would be responsible for a deceleration during return to the Earth to a residual speed that allows <a href="/wiki/Aerocapture" title="Aerocapture">aerocapture</a> of the spacecraft ending in a runway landing, much in the way of the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a>. One remarkable feature of this technical tale is that the engineer Wernher von Braun anticipated a medical phenomenon that would become apparent only years later: being a veteran astronaut with no history of serious adverse reactions to <a href="/wiki/Weightlessness" title="Weightlessness">weightlessness</a> offers no protection against becoming unexpectedly and violently <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spacesick" title="Spacesick">spacesick</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_conversion">Religious conversion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wernher_von_Braun&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Religious conversion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the first half of his life, von Braun was a nonpracticing, “perfunctory” Lutheran, whose affiliation was rather nominal and not taken seriously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> As described by <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Stuhlinger" title="Ernst Stuhlinger">Ernst Stuhlinger</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frederick_I._Ordway_III" title="Frederick I. Ordway III">Frederick I. Ordway III</a>: “Throughout his younger years, von Braun did not show signs of religious devotion, or even an interest in things related to the church or to biblical teachings. In fact, he was known to his friends as a 'merry heathen' (<i>fröhlicher Heide</i>).”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Nevertheless, in 1946,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-neu_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-neu-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:469</sup> he reluctantly attended church in <a href="/wiki/El_Paso,_Texas" title="El Paso, Texas">El Paso, Texas</a>, and underwent a <a href="/wiki/Religious_conversion" title="Religious conversion">religious conversion</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evangelical_Christianity" title="Evangelical Christianity">evangelical Christianity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rocket_Man_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rocket_Man-69">[69]</a></sup> In an unnamed religious magazine he stated:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>"One day in <a href="/wiki/Fort_Bliss" title="Fort Bliss">Fort Bliss</a>, a neighbor called and asked if I would like to go to church with him. I accepted, because I wanted to see if the American church was just a country club as I'd been led to expect. Instead, I found a small, white frame building... in the hot Texas sun on a browned-grass lot... Together, these people make a live, vibrant community. This was the first time I really understood that religion was not a cathedral inherited from the past, or a quick prayer at the last minute. To be effective, a religion has to be backed up by discipline and effort."</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— von Braun<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-neu_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-neu-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:229–230</sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>On the motives behind this conversion, <a href="/wiki/Michael_J._Neufeld" title="Michael J. Neufeld">Michael J. Neufeld</a> is of the opinion that he turned to religion “to pacify his own conscience”,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> whereas <a href="/wiki/University_of_Southampton" title="University of Southampton">Soton</a> scholar Kendrick Oliver comments that von Braun was presumably moved "by a desire to find a new direction for his life after the moral chaos of his service for the Third Reich.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Having "concluded one bad bargain with the devil, perhaps now he felt a need to have God securely at his side.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>Later in life, he joined an Episcopal congregation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rocket_Man_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rocket_Man-69">[69]</a></sup> and became increasingly religious.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> He publicly spoke and wrote about the complementarity of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Science_and_religion" title="Science and religion">science and religion</a>, the afterlife of the soul, and his belief in God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> He stated, "Through science man strives to learn more of the mysteries of creation. Through religion he seeks to know the Creator."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> He was interviewed by the <a href="/wiki/Assemblies_of_God" title="Assemblies of God">Assemblies of God</a> pastor C. M. Ward, as stating, “The farther we probe into space, the greater my faith.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> In addition, he met privately with evangelist <a href="/wiki/Billy_Graham" title="Billy Graham">Billy Graham</a> and with the pacifist leader Rev. <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King_Jr." title="Martin Luther King Jr.">Martin Luther King Jr.</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kennedy_vonbraun_19may63_02.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="734" data-file-width="541" height="298" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Kennedy_vonbraun_19may63_02.jpg/220px-Kennedy_vonbraun_19may63_02.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Kennedy_vonbraun_19may63_02.jpg/330px-Kennedy_vonbraun_19may63_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Kennedy_vonbraun_19may63_02.jpg/440px-Kennedy_vonbraun_19may63_02.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kennedy_vonbraun_19may63_02.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Von Braun with <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">President Kennedy</a> at Redstone Arsenal in 1963</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:S-IC_engines_and_Von_Braun.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="2415" height="273" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/S-IC_engines_and_Von_Braun.jpg/220px-S-IC_engines_and_Von_Braun.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/S-IC_engines_and_Von_Braun.jpg/330px-S-IC_engines_and_Von_Braun.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/S-IC_engines_and_Von_Braun.jpg/440px-S-IC_engines_and_Von_Braun.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
