</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:19700202-wernher-von-braun-nasa.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="2241" height="295" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/19700202-wernher-von-braun-nasa.jpg/220px-19700202-wernher-von-braun-nasa.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/19700202-wernher-von-braun-nasa.jpg/330px-19700202-wernher-von-braun-nasa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/19700202-wernher-von-braun-nasa.jpg/440px-19700202-wernher-von-braun-nasa.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:19700202-wernher-von-braun-nasa.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Still with his <a href="/wiki/Rocket" title="Rocket">rocket</a> <a href="/wiki/Scale_model" title="Scale model">models</a>, von Braun is pictured in his new office at NASA headquarters in 1970</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Concepts_for_orbital_warfare">Concepts for orbital warfare</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wernher_von_Braun&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Concepts for orbital warfare">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Von Braun developed and published his space station concept during the very "coldest" time of the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>, when the U.S. government for which he worked put the containment of the Soviet Union above everything else. The fact that his space station – if armed with missiles that could be easily adapted from those already available at this time – would give the United States space superiority in both orbital and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orbital_bombardment" title="Orbital bombardment">orbit-to-ground</a> warfare did not escape him. In his popular writings, von Braun elaborated on them in several of his books and articles, but he took care to qualify such military applications as "particularly dreadful". This much less peaceful aspect of von Braun's "drive for space" has been reviewed by Michael J. Neufeld from the Space History Division of the <a href="/wiki/National_Air_and_Space_Museum" title="National Air and Space Museum">National Air and Space Museum</a> in Washington.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="NASA_career">NASA career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wernher_von_Braun&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: NASA career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ap11-KSC-69P-632.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="804" data-file-width="640" height="276" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Ap11-KSC-69P-632.jpg/220px-Ap11-KSC-69P-632.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Ap11-KSC-69P-632.jpg/330px-Ap11-KSC-69P-632.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Ap11-KSC-69P-632.jpg/440px-Ap11-KSC-69P-632.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ap11-KSC-69P-632.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Von Braun during Apollo 11 launch</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">U.S. Navy</a> had been tasked with building a rocket to lift satellites into orbit, but the resulting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vanguard_rocket" title="Vanguard rocket">Vanguard rocket</a> launch system was unreliable. In 1957, with the launch of <a href="/wiki/Sputnik_1" title="Sputnik 1">Sputnik 1</a>, a growing belief within the United States existed that it was lagging behind the Soviet Union in the emerging <a href="/wiki/Space_Race" title="Space Race">Space Race</a>. American authorities then chose to use von Braun and his German team's experience with missiles to create an orbital launch vehicle. Wernher von Braun had such an idea originally proposed in 1954, but it was denied at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RM_55-7"><a href="#cite_note-RM-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>NASA was established by law on July 29, 1958. One day later, the 50th Redstone rocket was successfully launched from <a href="/wiki/Johnston_Atoll" title="Johnston Atoll">Johnston Atoll</a> in the south Pacific as part of <a href="/wiki/Operation_Hardtack_I" title="Operation Hardtack I">Operation Hardtack I</a>. Two years later, NASA opened the Marshall Space Flight Center at Redstone Arsenal in Huntsville, and the ABMA development team led by von Braun was transferred to NASA. In a face-to-face meeting with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herb_York" title="Herb York">Herb York</a> at the Pentagon, von Braun made it clear he would go to NASA only if development of the Saturn was allowed to continue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abma1_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-abma1-80">[80]</a></sup> Presiding from July 1960 to February 1970, von Braun became the center's first director.</p>
<p>Von Braun's early years at NASA were not without some disappointments. One of those was the "infamous four-inch flight" during which the first unmanned Mercury-Redstone rocket only rose a few inches before settling back onto the launch pad. The launch failure was later determined to be the result of a "power plug with one prong shorter than the other because a worker filed it to make it fit." Because of the difference in the length of one prong, the launch system detected the difference in the power disconnection as a "cut-off signal to the engine". The system stopped the launch, and the incident created a "nadir of morale in Project Mercury".</p>
<p>After the flight of <a href="/wiki/Mercury-Redstone_2" title="Mercury-Redstone 2">Mercury-Redstone 2</a> in January 1961 experienced a string of problems, von Braun insisted on one more test before the Redstone could be deemed man-rated. His overly cautious nature brought about clashes with other people involved in the program, who argued that MR-2's technical issues were simple and had been resolved shortly after the flight. He overruled them, so a test mission involving a Redstone on a boilerplate capsule was flown successfully in March. Von Braun's stubbornness was blamed for the inability of the U.S. to launch a manned space mission before the Soviet Union, which ended up putting the first man in space the following month.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_11_mission_officials_relax_after_Apollo_11_liftoff_-_GPN-2002-000026.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2181" data-file-width="2860" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Apollo_11_mission_officials_relax_after_Apollo_11_liftoff_-_GPN-2002-000026.jpg/220px-Apollo_11_mission_officials_relax_after_Apollo_11_liftoff_-_GPN-2002-000026.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Apollo_11_mission_officials_relax_after_Apollo_11_liftoff_-_GPN-2002-000026.jpg/330px-Apollo_11_mission_officials_relax_after_Apollo_11_liftoff_-_GPN-2002-000026.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Apollo_11_mission_officials_relax_after_Apollo_11_liftoff_-_GPN-2002-000026.jpg/440px-Apollo_11_mission_officials_relax_after_Apollo_11_liftoff_-_GPN-2002-000026.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_11_mission_officials_relax_after_Apollo_11_liftoff_-_GPN-2002-000026.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Charles W. Mathews, von Braun, <a href="/wiki/George_Mueller_(NASA)" title="George Mueller (NASA)">George Mueller</a>, and Lt. Gen. <a href="/wiki/Samuel_C._Phillips" title="Samuel C. Phillips">Samuel C. Phillips</a> in the Launch Control Center following the successful <a href="/wiki/Apollo_11" title="Apollo 11">Apollo 11</a> liftoff on July 16, 1969</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Marshall Center's first major program was the development of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saturn_rocket" title="Saturn rocket">Saturn rockets</a> to carry heavy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Payload_(air_and_space_craft)" title="Payload (air and space craft)">payloads</a> into and beyond <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth_orbit" title="Earth orbit">Earth orbit</a>. From this, the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">Apollo program</a> for manned Moon flights was developed. Wernher von Braun initially pushed for a flight engineering concept that called for an <a href="/wiki/Earth_orbit_rendezvous" title="Earth orbit rendezvous">Earth orbit rendezvous</a> technique (the approach he had argued for building his space station), but in 1962, he converted to the <a href="/wiki/Lunar_orbit_rendezvous" title="Lunar orbit rendezvous">lunar orbit rendezvous</a> concept that was subsequently realized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apmon_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-apmon-81">[81]</a></sup> During Apollo, he worked closely with former Peenemünde teammate, <a href="/wiki/Kurt_H._Debus" title="Kurt H. Debus">Kurt H. Debus</a>, the first director of the Kennedy Space Center. His dream to help mankind set foot on the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> became a reality on July 16, 1969, when a Marshall-developed <a href="/wiki/Saturn_V" title="Saturn V">Saturn V</a> rocket launched the crew of <a href="/wiki/Apollo_11" title="Apollo 11">Apollo 11</a> on its historic eight-day mission. Over the course of the program, Saturn V rockets enabled six teams of astronauts to reach the surface of the Moon.</p>
<p>During the late 1960s, von Braun was instrumental in the development of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Space_and_Rocket_Center" title="U.S. Space and Rocket Center">U.S. Space and Rocket Center</a> in Huntsville. The desk from which he guided America's entry in the space race remains on display there. He also was instrumental in the launching of the experimental <a href="/wiki/Applications_Technology_Satellite" title="Applications Technology Satellite">Applications Technology Satellite</a>. He travelled to <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> and hoped that the program would be helpful for bringing a massive <a href="/wiki/Educational_television" title="Educational television">educational television</a> project to help the poorest people in that country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the local summer of 1966–67, von Braun participated in a field trip to <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a>, organized for him and several other members of top NASA management.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> The goal of the field trip was to determine whether the experience gained by U.S. scientific and technological community during the exploration of Antarctic wastelands would be useful for the manned exploration of space. Von Braun was mainly interested in management of the scientific effort on Antarctic research stations, logistics, habitation, and life support, and in using the barren Antarctic terrain like the glacial dry valleys to test the equipment that one day would be used to look for signs of life on Mars and other worlds.</p>
<p>In an internal memo dated January 16, 1969,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-intan_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-intan-85">[85]</a></sup> von Braun had confirmed to his staff that he would stay on as a center director at Huntsville to head the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Applications_Program" title="Apollo Applications Program">Apollo Applications Program</a>. He referred to this time as a moment in his life when he felt the strong need to pray, stating "I certainly prayed a lot before and during the crucial Apollo flights".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> A few months later, on occasion of the first Moon landing, he publicly expressed his optimism that the Saturn V carrier system would continue to be developed, advocating manned missions to Mars in the 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>Nonetheless, on March 1, 1970, von Braun and his family relocated to Washington, DC, when he was assigned the post of NASA's Deputy Associate Administrator for Planning at NASA Headquarters. After a series of conflicts associated with the truncation of the Apollo program, and facing severe budget constraints, von Braun retired from NASA on May 26, 1972. Not only had it become evident by this time that NASA and his visions for future U.S. space flight projects were incompatible, but also it was perhaps even more frustrating for him to see popular support for a continued presence of man in space wane dramatically once the goal to reach the Moon had been accomplished.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:VonBraunLucas.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2377" data-file-width="3000" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/VonBraunLucas.jpg/220px-VonBraunLucas.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/VonBraunLucas.jpg/330px-VonBraunLucas.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/VonBraunLucas.jpg/440px-VonBraunLucas.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:VonBraunLucas.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Von Braun and <a href="/wiki/William_R._Lucas" title="William R. Lucas">William R. Lucas</a>, the first and third Marshall Space Flight Center directors, viewing a <a href="/wiki/Spacelab" title="Spacelab">Spacelab</a> model in 1974</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Dr. von Braun also developed the idea of a <a href="/wiki/United_States_Space_Camp" title="United States Space Camp">Space Camp</a> that would train children in fields of science and space technologies, as well as help their mental development much the same way sports camps aim at improving physical development. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neufeld_2007_18-6"><a href="#cite_note-Neufeld_2007-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:354–355</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Career_after_NASA">Career after NASA</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wernher_von_Braun&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Career after NASA">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After leaving NASA, von Braun became Vice President for Engineering and Development at the aerospace company, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fairchild_(aircraft_manufacturer)" title="Fairchild (aircraft manufacturer)">Fairchild Industries</a> in <a href="/wiki/Germantown,_Maryland" title="Germantown, Maryland">Germantown</a>, Maryland, on July 1, 1972.</p>
<p>In 1973, von Braun was diagnosed with <a href="/wiki/Renal_cell_carcinoma" title="Renal cell carcinoma">kidney cancer</a> during a routine physical examination, which could not be controlled with the medical techniques available at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Von Braun continued his work to the extent possible, which included accepting invitations to speak at colleges and universities, as he was eager to cultivate interest in human spaceflight and rocketry, particularly his desire to encourage the next generation of aerospace engineers.</p>
<p>Von Braun helped establish and promote the <a href="/wiki/National_Space_Institute" title="National Space Institute">National Space Institute</a>, a precursor of the present-day <a href="/wiki/National_Space_Society" title="National Space Society">National Space Society</a>, in 1975, and became its first president and chairman. In 1976, he became scientific consultant to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lutz_Kayser" title="Lutz Kayser">Lutz Kayser</a>, the CEO of <a href="/wiki/OTRAG" title="OTRAG">OTRAG</a>, and a member of the <a href="/wiki/Daimler-Benz" title="Daimler-Benz">Daimler-Benz</a> board of directors. However, his deteriorating health forced him to retire from Fairchild on December 31, 1976. When the 1975 <a href="/wiki/National_Medal_of_Science" title="National Medal of Science">National Medal of Science</a> was awarded to him in early 1977, he was hospitalized, and unable to attend the White House ceremony.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Engineering_philosophy">Engineering philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wernher_von_Braun&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Engineering philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Von Braun's insistence on further tests after <a href="/wiki/Mercury-Redstone_2" title="Mercury-Redstone 2">Mercury-Redstone 2</a> flew higher than planned has been identified as contributing to the Soviet Union's success in launching the first human in space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Mercury-Redstone_BD" title="Mercury-Redstone BD">Mercury-Redstone BD</a> flight was successful, but took up the launch slot that could have put <a href="/wiki/Alan_Shepard" title="Alan Shepard">Alan Shepard</a> into space three weeks ahead of <a href="/wiki/Yuri_Gagarin" title="Yuri Gagarin">Yuri Gagarin</a>. His Soviet counterpart <a href="/wiki/Sergei_Korolev" title="Sergei Korolev">Sergei Korolev</a> insisted on two successful flights with dogs before risking Gagarin's life on a manned attempt. The second test flight took place one day after the Mercury-Redstone BD mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neufeld_2007_18-7"><a href="#cite_note-Neufeld_2007-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:{{{1}}}</sup></p>
<p>Von Braun took a very conservative approach to engineering, designing with ample <a href="/wiki/Factor_of_safety" title="Factor of safety">safety factors</a> and <a href="/wiki/Redundancy_(engineering)" title="Redundancy (engineering)">redundant structure</a>. This became a point of contention with other engineers, who struggled to keep vehicle weight down so that payload could be maximized. As noted above, his excessive caution likely led to the U.S. losing the race to put a man into space with the Soviets. <a href="/wiki/Krafft_Arnold_Ehricke" title="Krafft Arnold Ehricke">Krafft Ehricke</a> likened von Braun's approach to building the <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn_Bridge" title="Brooklyn Bridge">Brooklyn Bridge</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_SP-4404_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_SP-4404-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:208</sup> Many at NASA headquarters jokingly referred to <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Space_Flight_Center" title="Marshall Space Flight Center">Marshall</a> as the "<a href="/wiki/Chicago_Bridge_%26_Iron_Company" title="Chicago Bridge &amp; Iron Company">Chicago Bridge and Iron Works</a>", but acknowledged that the designs worked.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOVA_1999_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-NOVA_1999-91">[91]</a></sup> The conservative approach paid off when a fifth engine was added to the <a href="/wiki/Saturn_C-4" title="Saturn C-4">Saturn C-4</a>, producing the <a href="/wiki/Saturn_V" title="Saturn V">Saturn V</a>. The C-4 design had a large crossbeam that could easily absorb the thrust of an additional engine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neufeld_2007_18-8"><a href="#cite_note-Neufeld_2007-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:371</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wernher_von_Braun&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Personal life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maria_von_Braun_6330121_edited.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="2365" height="279" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Maria_von_Braun_6330121_edited.jpg/220px-Maria_von_Braun_6330121_edited.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Maria_von_Braun_6330121_edited.jpg/330px-Maria_von_Braun_6330121_edited.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Maria_von_Braun_6330121_edited.jpg/440px-Maria_von_Braun_6330121_edited.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maria_von_Braun_6330121_edited.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Maria von Braun, wife of Wernher von Braun</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Von Braun had a charismatic personality and was known as a ladies' man. As a student in Berlin, he would often be seen in the evenings in the company of two girlfriends at once.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neufeld_2007_18-9"><a href="#cite_note-Neufeld_2007-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:63</sup> He later had a succession of affairs within the secretarial and <a href="/wiki/Human_computer" title="Human computer">computer</a> pool at Peenemünde.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neufeld_2007_18-10"><a href="#cite_note-Neufeld_2007-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:92–94</sup></p>
<p>In January 1943, he became engaged to Dorothee Brill, a physical education teacher in Berlin, and sought permission from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RuSHA" title="RuSHA">SS Race and Settlement Office</a> to marry. However, the engagement was broken due to his mother's opposition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neufeld_2007_18-11"><a href="#cite_note-Neufeld_2007-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:146–147</sup> Later in 1943, while preparing V-2 launch sites in northeastern France, von Braun had an affair in Paris with a Frenchwoman, who was imprisoned for collaboration after the War and became destitute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neufeld_2007_18-12"><a href="#cite_note-Neufeld_2007-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:147–148</sup></p>
<p>During his stay at Fort Bliss, von Braun proposed marriage to Maria Luise von Quistorp (born <span style="display:none">(<span class="bday">1928-06-10</span>)</span>June 10, 1928), his maternal first cousin, in a letter to his father. On March 1, 1947, having received permission to go back to Germany and return with his bride, he married her in a <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheran church</a> in <a href="/wiki/Landshut" title="Landshut">Landshut</a>, Germany. Shortly after he converted to <a href="/wiki/Evangelicalism" title="Evangelicalism">Evangelical Christianity</a>, his bride and he, as well as his father and mother, returned to New York on March 26, 1947. On December 9, 1948, the von Brauns' first daughter, Iris Careen, was born at Fort Bliss Army Hospital.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-time58_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-time58-59">[59]</a></sup> The von Brauns had two more children, Margrit Cécile in 1952 and Peter Constantine in 1960.</p>
<p>On April 15, 1955, von Braun became a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naturalized_citizen" title="Naturalized citizen">naturalized citizen</a> of the United States.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wernher_von_Braun&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Von_Braun_Wernher_grave.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1125" data-file-width="1577" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Von_Braun_Wernher_grave.jpg/220px-Von_Braun_Wernher_grave.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Von_Braun_Wernher_grave.jpg/330px-Von_Braun_Wernher_grave.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Von_Braun_Wernher_grave.jpg/440px-Von_Braun_Wernher_grave.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Von_Braun_Wernher_grave.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Grave of Wernher von Braun in <a href="/wiki/Ivy_Hill_Cemetery_(Alexandria,_Virginia)" title="Ivy Hill Cemetery (Alexandria, Virginia)">Ivy Hill Cemetery</a> (Alexandria, Virginia), 2008.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On June 16, 1977, Wernher von Braun died of <a href="/wiki/Pancreatic_cancer" title="Pancreatic cancer">pancreatic cancer</a> in <a href="/wiki/Alexandria,_Virginia" title="Alexandria, Virginia">Alexandria</a>, Virginia, at the age of 65.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> He was buried at the <a href="/wiki/Ivy_Hill_Cemetery_(Alexandria,_Virginia)" title="Ivy Hill Cemetery (Alexandria, Virginia)">Ivy Hill Cemetery</a> in <a href="/wiki/Alexandria,_Virginia" title="Alexandria, Virginia">Alexandria</a>, Virginia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>Von Braun's gravestone mentions Psalms 19:1: "The heavens declare the glory of God; and the firmament sheweth his handywork.(KJV)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recognition_and_critique">Recognition and critique</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wernher_von_Braun&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Recognition and critique">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:VonBraunFamily.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2347" data-file-width="3000" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/VonBraunFamily.jpg/220px-VonBraunFamily.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/VonBraunFamily.jpg/330px-VonBraunFamily.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/VonBraunFamily.jpg/440px-VonBraunFamily.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:VonBraunFamily.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
In 1970, <a href="/wiki/Huntsville,_Alabama" title="Huntsville, Alabama">Huntsville, Alabama</a> honored von Braun's years of service with a series of events including the unveiling of a plaque in his honor. Pictured (l–r), his daughter Iris, wife <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maria_Luise_von_Quistorp" title="Maria Luise von Quistorp">Maria</a>, U.S. Sen. <a href="/wiki/John_Sparkman" title="John Sparkman">John Sparkman</a>, Alabama Gov. <a href="/wiki/Albert_Brewer" title="Albert Brewer">Albert Brewer</a>, von Braun, son Peter, and daughter Margrit.</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">Apollo program</a> director <a href="/wiki/Samuel_C._Phillips" title="Samuel C. Phillips">Sam Phillips</a> was quoted as saying that he did not think that the United States would have reached the Moon as quickly as it did without von Braun's help. Later, after discussing it with colleagues, he amended this to say that he did not believe the United States would have reached the Moon at all.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ward_2005_11-6"><a href="#cite_note-Ward_2005-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:167</sup></li>
<li>The crater <a href="/wiki/Von_Braun_(crater)" title="Von Braun (crater)">von Braun</a> on the Moon is named after him.</li>
<li>Von Braun received a total of 12 honorary doctorates, among them, on January 8, 1963, one from the <a href="/wiki/Technical_University_of_Berlin" title="Technical University of Berlin">Technical University of Berlin</a> from which he had graduated.</li>
<li>Von Braun was responsible for the creation of the Research Institute at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Alabama_in_Huntsville" title="University of Alabama in Huntsville">University of Alabama in Huntsville</a>. As a result of his vision, the university is one of the leading universities in the nation for NASA-sponsored research. The building housing the university's Research Institute was named in his honor, Von Braun Research Hall, in 2000.</li>
<li>Several German cities (<a href="/wiki/Bonn" title="Bonn">Bonn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neu-Isenburg" title="Neu-Isenburg">Neu-Isenburg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mannheim" title="Mannheim">Mannheim</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mainz" title="Mainz">Mainz</a>), and dozens of smaller towns have named streets after Wernher von Braun.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Von_Braun_Center" title="Von Braun Center">Von Braun Center</a> (built 1975) in Huntsville is named in von Braun's honor.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Von_Braun_Astronomical_Society" title="Von Braun Astronomical Society">Von Braun Astronomical Society</a> in Huntsville was originally founded as the Rocket city Astronomical Association by von Braun and was later renamed after him</li>
<li>Scrutiny of von Braun's use of forced labor at <a href="/wiki/Mittelwerk" title="Mittelwerk">Mittelwerk</a> intensified again in 1984 when <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Rudolph" title="Arthur Rudolph">Arthur Rudolph</a>, one of his top affiliates from the A-4/V2 through to the Apollo projects, left the United States and was forced to renounce his citizenship in place of the alternative of being tried for war crimes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></li>
<li>A science- and engineering-oriented <a href="/wiki/Gymnasium_(school)" title="Gymnasium (school)">Gymnasium</a> in <a href="/wiki/Friedberg,_Bavaria" title="Friedberg, Bavaria">Friedberg, Bavaria</a> was named after Wernher von Braun in 1979. In response to rising criticism, a school committee decided in 1995, after lengthy deliberations, to keep the name but "to address von Braun's ambiguity in the advanced history classes". In 2012, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nazi_concentration_camp" title="Nazi concentration camp">Nazi concentration camp</a> survivor David Salz gave a speech in Friedberg, calling out for the public to "Do everything to make this name disappear from this school!".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> In February 2014, the school was finally renamed "Staatliches Gymnasium Friedberg" and distanced itself from the name von Braun, citing he was "no role-model for our pupils".</li>
<li>An avenue in the Annadale section of <a href="/wiki/Staten_Island" title="Staten Island">Staten Island</a>, New York was named after him in 1977.</li>
<li>Von Braun also was voted into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Space_and_Rocket_Center" title="U.S. Space and Rocket Center">U.S. Space and Rocket Center</a> Hall of Fame, 2007</li>
</ul>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Summary_of_SS_career">Summary of SS career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wernher_von_Braun&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Summary of SS career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li>SS number: 185,068</li>
<li>Nazi Party number: 5,738,692<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neufeld_2007_18-13"><a href="#cite_note-Neufeld_2007-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:96</sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dates_of_rank">Dates of rank</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wernher_von_Braun&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Dates of rank">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>SS-<a href="/wiki/Anw%C3%A4rter" title="Anwärter">Anwärter</a>: November 1, 1933 (<i>Candidate; received rank upon joining SS Riding School</i>)</li>
<li>SS-<a href="/wiki/Mann_(military_rank)" title="Mann (military rank)">Mann</a>: July 1934 (Private)</li>
</ul>
<p>(<i>left SS after graduation from the school; commissioned in 1940 with date of entry backdated to 1934</i>)</p>
<ul>
<li>SS-<a href="/wiki/Untersturmf%C3%BChrer" title="Untersturmführer">Untersturmführer</a>: May 1, 1940 (Second Lieutenant)</li>
<li>SS-<a href="/wiki/Obersturmf%C3%BChrer" title="Obersturmführer">Obersturmführer</a>: November 9, 1941 (First Lieutenant)</li>
<li>SS-<a href="/wiki/Hauptsturmf%C3%BChrer" title="Hauptsturmführer">Hauptsturmführer</a>: November 9, 1942 (Captain)</li>
<li>SS-<a href="/wiki/Sturmbannf%C3%BChrer" title="Sturmbannführer">Sturmbannführer</a>: June 28, 1943 (Major)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astronautix_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-astronautix-25">[25]</a></sup></li>
</ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wernher_von_Braun&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: In popular culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<dl>
<dt>Film and television</dt>
</dl>
<p>Von Braun has been featured in a number of movies and television shows or series:</p>
<ul>
<li>"<a href="/wiki/Man_in_Space" title="Man in Space">Man in Space</a>", an episode of <i>Disneyland</i> which originally aired on March 9, 1955.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/I_Aim_at_the_Stars" title="I Aim at the Stars">I Aim at the Stars</a></i> (1960), also titled <i>Wernher von Braun</i> and <i>Ich greife nach den Sternen</i> ("I Reach for the Stars"); von Braun played by <a href="/wiki/Curd_J%C3%BCrgens" title="Curd Jürgens">Curd Jürgens</a>, his wife Maria played by <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Shaw_(actress)" title="Victoria Shaw (actress)">Victoria Shaw</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Satirist <a href="/wiki/Mort_Sahl" title="Mort Sahl">Mort Sahl</a> suggested the subtitle "But Sometimes I Hit London".</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/From_the_Earth_to_the_Moon_(miniseries)" title="From the Earth to the Moon (miniseries)">From the Earth to the Moon</a></i> (TV, 1998): von Braun played by <a href="/wiki/Norbert_Weisser" title="Norbert Weisser">Norbert Weisser</a>.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/October_Sky" title="October Sky">October Sky</a></i> (1999): this film portrays U.S. rocket scientist <a href="/wiki/Homer_Hickam" title="Homer Hickam">Homer Hickam</a>, who as a teenager admired von Braun (played by Joe Digaetano). The film's title, <i>October Sky</i>, is an <a href="/wiki/Anagram" title="Anagram">anagram</a> of the autobiography it was based on: <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rocket_Boys" title="Rocket Boys">Rocket Boys</a></i>.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Space_Race_(TV_series)" title="Space Race (TV series)">Space Race</a></i> (TV, <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a> co-production with <a href="/wiki/Norddeutscher_Rundfunk" title="Norddeutscher Rundfunk">NDR</a> (Germany), Channel One TV (Russia) and <a href="/wiki/National_Geographic_Society" title="National Geographic Society">National Geographic</a> TV (USA), 2005): von Braun played by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Dillane" title="Richard Dillane">Richard Dillane</a>.</li>
<li><i>The Lost Von Braun</i>, a documentary by Aron Ranen. Interviews with Ernst Stuhlinger, Konrad Dannenberg, Karl Sendler, Alex Baum, Eli Rosenbaum (DOJ) and von Braun's NASA secretary Bonnie Holmes.</li>
<li><i><a class="external text" href="http://www.dw-world.de/dw/episode/0,2144,2617731,00.html" rel="nofollow">Wernher von Braun – Rocket Man for War and Peace</a></i> A three part (<a class="external text" href="http://www.dw-world.de/dw/episode/0,2144,2617731,00.html" rel="nofollow">part1</a>, <a class="external text" href="http://www.dw-world.de/dw/episode/0,2144,2617732,00.html" rel="nofollow">part 2</a>, <a class="external text" href="http://www.dw-world.de/dw/episode/0,2144,2617733,00.html" rel="nofollow">part 3</a>) documentary – in English – from the German International channel <a href="/wiki/DW-TV" title="DW-TV">DW-TV</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Original German version <a class="external text" href="http://www.mdr.de/doku/archiv/geschichte/109389.html" rel="nofollow">Wernher von Braun – Der Mann für die Wunderwaffen</a> by the Mitteldeutscher Rundfunk.</li>
<li>The episode "Pursuit" of the 1982 television series <i><a href="/wiki/Voyagers!" title="Voyagers!">Voyagers!</a></i> involves the lead characters, Bogg and Jeffrey, attempting to connect Wernher von Braun with American army forces as the German regime crumbles between Hitler's death and the surrender. Jeffrey had discovered an altered history in which the Soviets reached the Moon while American rockets exploded on the launch pad, and learned that von Braun's team had been captured by the Soviet forces due to a traitor in their midst. David Olivier portrayed von Braun.</li>
<li>The episode "Party at Castle Varlar" of the 2016 television series <i><a href="/wiki/Timeless_(TV_series)" title="Timeless (TV series)">Timeless</a></i> involves a time travel to 1944 and rises questions of justice and responsibility regarding von Braun's work and fate.</li>
</ul>
<p>Several fictional characters have been modeled on von Braun:</p>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Dr._Strangelove" title="Dr. Strangelove">Dr. Strangelove or: How I Learned to Stop Worrying and Love the Bomb</a></i> (1964): Dr Strangelove is usually held to be based at least partly on von Braun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></li>
<li>In <i><a href="/wiki/Diamonds_Are_Forever_(film)" title="Diamonds Are Forever (film)">Diamonds Are Forever</a></i> (1971), the seventh <a href="/wiki/James_Bond" title="James Bond">James Bond</a> film, the supervillain <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Stavro_Blofeld" title="Ernst Stavro Blofeld">Ernst Stavro Blofeld</a> employs a German scientist resembling von Braun, named <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_James_Bond_henchmen_in_Diamonds_Are_Forever#Professor_Dr._Metz" title="List of James Bond henchmen in Diamonds Are Forever">Professor Dr. Metz</a>, a pacifist who is duped by Blofeld's rhetoric, who works in a NASA-style research lab in the Nevada desert. The lab is ambiguously depicted as being involved in <a href="/wiki/Moon_landing_conspiracy_theories" title="Moon landing conspiracy theories">faking the Moon landings</a> (an apparent joking nod to conspiracy theorists, and which figures in some versions of their theories).</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dt>Print media</dt>
</dl>
<ul>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/Warren_Ellis" title="Warren Ellis">Warren Ellis</a>'s graphic novel <i><a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Space" title="Ministry of Space">Ministry of Space</a></i>, von Braun is a supporting character, settling in Britain after World War II, and being essential for the realization of the British Space Program.</li>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Hickman" title="Jonathan Hickman">Jonathan Hickman</a>'s comic book series <i><a href="/wiki/The_Manhattan_Projects" title="The Manhattan Projects">The Manhattan Projects</a></i>, von Braun is a major character, joining the Manhattan Projects following his killing of his Nazi brethren. He is also depicted with a robotic arm that he received from Hitler for his good work with the Nazis.</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dt>Literature</dt>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Good_German" title="The Good German">The Good German</a></i> by Joseph Kanon. Von Braun and other scientists are said to have been implicated in the use of slave labour at Peenemünde; their transfer to the U.S. forms part of the narrative.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Space_(novel)" title="Space (novel)">Space</a></i> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_Michener" title="James Michener">James Michener</a>. Von Braun and other German scientists are brought to the U.S. and form a vital part of the U.S. efforts to reach space.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Gravity%27s_Rainbow" title="Gravity's Rainbow">Gravity's Rainbow</a></i> by Thomas Pynchon. The novel involved British intelligence attempting to avert and predict V-2 rocket attacks. The work even includes a gyroscopic equation for the V2. The first portion of the novel, "Beyond The Zero", begins with a quote from von Braun: "Nature does not know extinction; all it knows is transformation. Everything science has taught me, and continues to teach me, strengthens my belief in the continuity of our spiritual existence after death."</li>
<li><i>V-S Day</i> by <a href="/wiki/Allen_Steele" title="Allen Steele">Allen Steele</a> is a 2014 <a href="/wiki/Alternate_history" title="Alternate history">alternate history</a> novel in which the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_race" title="Space race">space race</a> occurs during World War II between teams led by <a href="/wiki/Robert_H._Goddard" title="Robert H. Goddard">Robert H. Goddard</a> and von Braun.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Moonglow:_A_Novel" title="Moonglow: A Novel">Moonglow</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Michael_Chabon" title="Michael Chabon">Michael Chabon</a> (2016) includes a fictionalized description of the search for and capture of Von Braun by the US Army, and his role in the Nazi V-2 program and subsequently in the US Space Program.</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dt>Theatre</dt>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><i>Rocket Girl</i>, a stage play by George D. Morgan, deals with the invention of <a href="/wiki/Hydyne" title="Hydyne">hydyne</a>, a special fuel designed to boost Explorer I, America's first satellite, into orbit utilizing the Redstone/Jupiter-C. Von Braun is not a character in the play, but is frequently mentioned in the dialogue.</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dt>Music</dt>
</dl>
<ul>
<li>"Wernher von Braun" (1965):<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> A song written and performed by <a href="/wiki/Tom_Lehrer" title="Tom Lehrer">Tom Lehrer</a> for an episode of <a href="/wiki/NBC" title="NBC">NBC</a>'s American version of the BBC TV show <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/That_Was_The_Week_That_Was" title="That Was The Week That Was">That Was The Week That Was</a></i>; the song was later included in Lehrer's album <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/That_Was_The_Year_That_Was" title="That Was The Year That Was">That Was The Year That Was</a></i>. It was a satire on what some saw as von Braun's cavalier attitude toward the consequences of his work in Nazi Germany: "'Once the rockets are up, who cares where they come down? / That's not my department', says Wernher von Braun." Contrary to popular belief, Wernher von Braun did not sue Tom Lehrer for defamation, nor has Lehrer been forced to relinquish all of his royalty income to von Braun. Lehrer firmly denied those claims in a 2003 interview.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>The Last Days of Pompeii</i> (1991): A rock opera by <a href="/wiki/Grant_Hart" title="Grant Hart">Grant Hart</a>'s post-<a href="/wiki/H%C3%BCsker_D%C3%BC" title="Hüsker Dü">Hüsker Dü</a> alternative rock group <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nova_Mob" title="Nova Mob">Nova Mob</a>, in which von Braun features as a character. The album includes a song called "Wernher von Braun".</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Published_works">Published works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wernher_von_Braun&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Published works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li><cite class="citation book"><i>Proposal for a Workable Fighter with Rocket Drive</i>. July 6, 1939.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AWernher+von+Braun&amp;rft.btitle=Proposal+for+a+Workable+Fighter+with+Rocket+Drive&amp;rft.date=1939-07-06&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span>
<ul>
<li>The proposed vertical take-off interceptor<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Klee_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Klee-108">[108]</a></sup> for climbing to 35,000 ft in 60 seconds was rejected by the Luftwaffe in the autumn of 1941<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dornberger_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dornberger-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:258</sup> for the <a href="/wiki/Messerschmitt_Me_163_Komet" title="Messerschmitt Me 163 Komet">Messerschmitt Me 163 Komet</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neufeld_2007_18-14"><a href="#cite_note-Neufeld_2007-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:151</sup> and never produced. (The differing <a href="/wiki/Bachem_Ba_349" title="Bachem Ba 349">Bachem Ba 349</a> was produced during the 1944 <a href="/wiki/Emergency_Fighter_Program" title="Emergency Fighter Program">Emergency Fighter Program</a>.)</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><cite class="citation book"><i>'Survey' of Previous Liquid Rocket Development in Germany and Future Prospects</i>. May 1945.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AWernher+von+Braun&amp;rft.btitle=%27Survey%27+of+Previous+Liquid+Rocket+Development+in+Germany+and+Future+Prospects&amp;rft.date=1945-05&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ordway_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ordway-109">[109]</a></sup></li>
<li><cite class="citation book"><i>A Minimum Satellite Vehicle Based on Components Available from Developments of the Army Ordnance Corps</i>. September 15, 1954. <q><i>It would be a blow to U.S. prestige if we did not [launch a satellite] first</i>.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AWernher+von+Braun&amp;rft.btitle=A+Minimum+Satellite+Vehicle+Based+on+Components+Available+from+Developments+of+the+Army+Ordnance+Corps&amp;rft.date=1954-09-15&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ordway_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ordway-109">[109]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Mars_Project" title="The Mars Project">The Mars Project</a></i>, Urbana, University of Illinois Press, (1953). With Henry J. White, translator.</li>
<li><cite class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/Arthur_C._Clarke" title="Arthur C. Clarke">Arthur C. Clarke</a>, ed. (1967). <i>German Rocketry, The Coming of the Space Age</i>. New York: Meredith Press.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AWernher+von+Braun&amp;rft.btitle=German+Rocketry%2C+The+Coming+of+the+Space+Age&amp;rft.date=1967&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.place=New+York&amp;rft.pub=Meredith+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/First_Men_to_the_Moon" title="First Men to the Moon">First Men to the Moon</a></i>, Holt, Rinehart and Winston, New York (1958). Portions of work first appeared in <i><a href="/wiki/This_Week_(magazine)" title="This Week (magazine)">This Week</a></i> Magazine.</li>
<li><cite class="citation book"><i>Daily Journals of Werner von Braun, May 1958 – March 1970</i>. March 1970.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AWernher+von+Braun&amp;rft.btitle=Daily+Journals+of+Werner+von+Braun%2C+May+1958+%93+March+1970&amp;rft.date=1970-03&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ordway_109-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ordway-109">[109]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>History of Rocketry &amp; Space Travel</i>, New York, Crowell (1975). With Frederick I. Ordway III.
<ul>
<li><cite class="citation book">Estate of Wernher von Braun; <a href="/wiki/Frederick_I._Ordway_III" title="Frederick I. Ordway III">Ordway III, Frederick I</a> &amp; Dooling, David Jr. (1985) [1975]. <i>Space Travel: A History</i> (2nd ed.). New York: Harper &amp; Row. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-06-181898-4" title="Special:BookSources/0-06-181898-4">0-06-181898-4</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AWernher+von+Braun&amp;rft.au=Dooling%2C+David+Jr.&amp;rft.au=Estate+of+Wernher+von+Braun&amp;rft.au=Ordway+III%2C+Frederick+I&amp;rft.btitle=Space+Travel%3A+A+History&amp;rft.date=1985&amp;rft.edition=2nd&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=0-06-181898-4&amp;rft.place=New+York&amp;rft.pub=Harper+%26+Row&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><i>The Rocket's Red Glare</i>, Garden City, N.Y.: Anchor Press, (1976). With Frederick I. Ordway III.</li>
<li><i>Project Mars: A Technical Tale</i>, Apogee Books, Toronto (2006). A previously unpublished science fiction story by von Braun. Accompanied by paintings from <a href="/wiki/Chesley_Bonestell" title="Chesley Bonestell">Chesley Bonestell</a> and von Braun's own technical papers on the proposed project.</li>
<li><i>The Voice of Dr. Wernher von Braun</i>, Apogee Books, Toronto (2007). A collection of speeches delivered by von Braun over the course of his career.</li>
</ul>

<li id="cite_note-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-13">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Various sources such as <a class="external text" href="http://www.questia.com/read/9806294?title=The%20Nazi%20Rocketeers%3a%20Dreams%20of%20Space%20and%20Crimes%20of%20War" rel="nofollow">The Nazi Rocketeers</a> (<a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0811733874">ISBN 0811733874</a> pp 5–8) list the young Wernher von Braun as joining the VfR as an apprentice to Willy Ley, one of the three founders. Later when Ley fled Germany because he was a Jew, von Braun took over the leadership of the <i>Verein</i> and changed its activity to military development.</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-87"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-87">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation news"><a class="external text" href="http://www.time.com/time/magazine/article/0,9171,901107,00.html" rel="nofollow">"Next, Mars and Beyond"</a>. <a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time (magazine)</a>. July 25, 1969<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">June 21,</span> 2007</span>. <q>Even as man prepared to take his first tentative extraterrestrial steps, other celestial adventures beckoned him. The shape and scope of the post-Apollo manned space program remained hazy, and a great deal depends on the safe and successful outcome of <a href="/wiki/Apollo_11" title="Apollo 11">Apollo 11</a>. Well before the lunar flight was launched, though, <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> was casting eyes on targets far beyond the Moon. The most inviting: the earth's close, and probably most hospitable, planetary neighbor. Given the same energy and dedication that took them to the Moon, says Wernher von Braun, Americans could land on Mars as early as 1982.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AWernher+von+Braun&amp;rft.atitle=Next%2C+Mars+and+Beyond&amp;rft.date=1969-07-25&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.time.com%2Ftime%2Fmagazine%2Farticle%2F0%2C9171%2C901107%2C00.html&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-88"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-88">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">German sources mostly specify the cancer as renal, while American biographies unanimously just mention cancer. The time when von Braun learned about the disease is generally given as between 1973 and 1976. The characteristics of renal cell carcinoma, which has a bad prognosis even today, do not rule out either time limit.</span></li>
